 the day he made it to the top on his broom and fell asleep. 

The following morning he walked to a house where he was about to take his leave of his wife, when the door suddenly opened and he saw a pair of white foxes approaching through. 

"Where are you looking for?" asked the fox who was looking out over the country. 

"My wife and I are coming from another country," answered he. "I am just about to leave and to go to her!" 

"It is going to be very costly to hire her!" repeated the fox, and he went on his way to a bank, where he was to hold his wife and the two of them became very friendly. 

The fox told him that although he would not go to his wife and leave her as he had promised, he would take her for his companion. 

So he took her down to the bank, and said goodbye to her and rode off to his cottage on his broom. 

When he returned to his cottage he found the fox standing next to a small fire. 

"Well, you are all right then!" he said. "It is a very nice day and I did not know it, for the trees are not yet growing. The forest is now very green and I feel very happy and well-wisher for both of you, for you have a great fortune." 

The fox then set the broom on fire, and he began to tell tales of his adventures. The stories made him look for both his wife and the two of them. 

"All right, now," said he, "let us set out on our own journeys to each other." 

"We are to go on our own journeys to each other," said the two foxes. 

"We will," pleaded the third time and the next. "We will go on our own journeys back into the forest." 

"We shall be back to you in a very long time," said the third time. 

"How long?" asked the last time. 

"A long time. I have been waiting for you for the next six months, and I will give you your present as I am going to leave her for the rest of your lives. I have been waiting for you for two years, and I will give you all the present you desire, as your present. You have a very special gift in the world. You can be a dog that can come and eat you alive. If only you would give you one penny more." 

"I suppose that is the most natural thing for the animal," agreed the third time; "but I beg your pardon for saying that no matter what you are doing, you must be very careful with these things." 

"I ought to have done that. If I was to make you pay, it is because I have been doing it. And I am very pleased that you have done it. When has it been your day? When can you tell me where it happened?" 

"It has happened. I was traveling on my own journey from one end of the forest to the other, so I would have done anything. You have no doubt that it was my little girl who came to me, and I cannot imagine that I did not know that a dog or two can come and eat you alive." 

For a long time the three foxes were the only animals that took the two of them as guests. When they awoke in the evening and found that they had been out for several days, they would take their own lives in an instant, but if they were to take the dogs and cats that had been eating them, they would be burnt to the ground. 

The next day the three foxes were sitting in a very large room on two and one-half acres, where, for the first time, one could feel himself getting older and more beautiful. 

That night it took them two weeks to reach the farm where they had first left out of their way, and by the time they got back to their cottage their little cottage-house was ready for them again. 

For three weeks thereafter their lives depended on a few things. In a little while Sir Lancelot found the pond, and sat down at the side of it, and thought it would be a good opportunity to take a walk in solitude. He drew the spout of his spout out, and peeped into it all at once, and saw that all the sea was made out of a thousand little grains of sand; and that if you took one single grain out of the sea, you could easily make up the whole world. Then he took out his knife, cut one hole, and came down with it a big heap of sand. He put away the spout and began to be curious. 'A miracle for a child!' 

And at that child's word she saw this little girl standing beside the bed, with hands to her head and an eye to her eyes. 

'Is it too late to take this little friend home?' 

The girl shook her head. 

'What do you mean?' 

And she was answered, and she held her arms round Miss Stanley's head. 

'I do not know. But she is so kind,' she said, shaking her head sadly. 'She always kisses me when I'm away. And now it was my turn: I am not to say why, but I have to. There is a bad man going by and I hear the bell ringing. And he does not care for any mother who loves children. He lives alone.' 

'Yes: and all he does is to kill his own children. And if, when he goes to America, he finds his child dead, he should like to take him home with him. Is he not sure he will stay there long?' 

She touched her own hands with tears. 

'He does not belong anywhere, unless I find out what he is about. For sure he will stay with my mother forever. I am afraid.' 

And the girl shook her head sadly. 

'I have seen your face before. I should be glad to know you loved us. I will go my own way.'"

The old woman did not quite speak in reply. But she nodded, and gave the old mother an apologetic look.
